Gold, 79Au

**Gold** is a [chemical element](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_element "Chemical element"); its [chemical symbol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_symbol "Chemical symbol") is **Au** (from [Latin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin "Latin") _aurum_) and [atomic number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_number "Atomic number") 79\. In its pure form, it is a [bright](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brightness "Brightness")-metallic- [yellow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yellow "Yellow"), dense, soft, [malleable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malleable "Malleable"), and [ductile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ductile "Ductile") [metal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metal "Metal"). Chemically, gold is a [transition metal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transition_metal "Transition metal"), a [group 11 element](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Group_11_element "Group 11 element"), and one of the [noble metals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noble_metal "Noble metal"). It is one of the least [reactive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reactivity_(chemistry) "Reactivity (chemistry)") chemical elements, being the second lowest in the [reactivity series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reactivity_series "Reactivity series"), with only [platinum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platinum "Platinum") ranked as less reactive.[\[10\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-10) Gold is solid under [standard conditions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure "Standard temperature and pressure").

Gold often occurs as the [free element](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_element "Free element") ( [native state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Native_state_(metallurgy) "Native state (metallurgy)")), as [nuggets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_nugget "Gold nugget") or grains, in [rocks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rock_(geology) "Rock (geology)"), [veins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vein_(geology) "Vein (geology)"), and [alluvial deposits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alluvial_deposit "Alluvial deposit"). It occurs in a [solid solution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid_solution "Solid solution") series with the native element [silver](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silver "Silver") (as in [electrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrum "Electrum")), naturally [alloyed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alloy "Alloy") with other metals such as [copper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copper "Copper"), platinum, and [palladium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palladium "Palladium"), as well as [mineral inclusions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mineral_inclusion "Mineral inclusion") such as within [pyrite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrite "Pyrite"). Less commonly, it occurs in minerals as gold compounds, often with [tellurium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tellurium "Tellurium") ( [gold tellurides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_telluride "Gold telluride")).

Gold is resistant to most acids, though it does dissolve in [aqua regia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aqua_regia "Aqua regia") (a mixture of [nitric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_acid "Nitric acid") and [hydrochloric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid "Hydrochloric acid")), forming a soluble [tetrachloroaurate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrachloroaurate "Tetrachloroaurate") [anion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anion "Anion"). Gold is insoluble in nitric acid alone, which dissolves silver and [base metals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Base_metal "Base metal"), a property long used to [refine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Refining_(metallurgy) "Refining (metallurgy)") gold and confirm the presence of gold in metallic substances, giving rise to the term " [acid test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acid_test_(gold) "Acid test (gold)")". Gold dissolves in [alkaline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alkaline "Alkaline") solutions of [cyanide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanide "Cyanide"), which are used in [mining](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_cyanidation "Gold cyanidation") and [electroplating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electroplating "Electroplating"). Gold also dissolves in [mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(element) "Mercury (element)"), forming [amalgam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amalgam_(chemistry) "Amalgam (chemistry)") alloys, and as the gold acts simply as a solute, this is not a [chemical reaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_reaction "Chemical reaction").

A relatively rare element when compared to silver[\[11\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-11)[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-12) (though thirty times more common than platinum),[\[13\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-13) gold is a [precious metal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precious_metal "Precious metal") that has been used for [coinage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_coin "Gold coin"), [jewelry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewelry "Jewelry"), and other [works of art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Works_of_art "Works of art") throughout [recorded history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recorded_history "Recorded history"). In the past, a [gold standard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_standard "Gold standard") was often implemented as a [monetary policy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monetary_policy "Monetary policy"). Most gold coins ceased to be minted as a circulating currency in the 1930s, and the world gold standard was abandoned for a [fiat currency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiat_currency "Fiat currency") system after the [Nixon shock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nixon_shock "Nixon shock") measures of 1971.

In 2023, the [world's largest gold producer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_gold_production "List of countries by gold production") was China, followed by Russia and Australia.[\[14\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Gold_Production-2023-14) As of 2020[\[update\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Gold&action=edit), a total of around 201,296 [tonnes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tonne "Tonne") of gold exist above ground.[\[15\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-15) If all of this gold were put together into a cube shape, each of its sides would measure 21.7 meters (71 ft). The world's consumption of new gold produced is about 50% in jewelry, 40% in [investments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_as_an_investment "Gold as an investment"), and 10% in [industry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manufacturing "Manufacturing").[\[16\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Soos-2011-16) Gold's high malleability, ductility, resistance to corrosion and most other chemical reactions, as well as conductivity of electricity have led to its continued use in corrosion-resistant [electrical connectors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_connector "Electrical connector") in all types of computerized devices (its chief industrial use). Gold is also used in [infrared](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared "Infrared") shielding, the production of [colored glass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colored_glass "Colored glass"), [gold leafing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_leaf "Gold leaf"), and [tooth restoration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Restorative_dentistry "Restorative dentistry"). [Auranofin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auranofin "Auranofin") is a gold-containing drug used to treat [rheumatoid arthritis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rheumatoid_arthritis "Rheumatoid arthritis").

## Etymology

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d5/Beowulf_-_gold.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Beowulf_-_gold.jpg) The word _gold_ in the [_Beowulf_ manuscript](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nowell_Codex "Nowell Codex")

_Gold_ is [cognate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognate "Cognate") with similar words in many [Germanic languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germanic_languages "Germanic languages"), deriving via [Proto-Germanic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Germanic "Proto-Germanic") [\* _gulþą_](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Germanic/gul%C3%BE%C4%85 "wikt:Appendix:Proto-Germanic/gulþą") from [Proto-Indo-European](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Indo-European "Proto-Indo-European") [\* _ǵʰelh₃-_](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/%C7%B5%CA%B0elh%E2%82%83- "wikt:Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/ǵʰelh₃-")'to shine, to gleam; to be yellow or green'.[\[17\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-17)[\[18\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-18)

The symbol _Au_ is from the [Latin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin "Latin") _[aurum](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aurum "wikt:aurum")_'gold'.[\[19\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-19) The Proto-Indo-European ancestor of _aurum_ was _\*h₂é-h₂us-o-_, meaning 'glow'. This word is derived from the same [root](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Root_(linguistics) "Root (linguistics)") (Proto-Indo-European _\*h₂u̯es-_'to dawn') as [_\*h₂éu̯sōs_](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/h%E2%82%82%C3%A9ws%C5%8Ds "wikt:Appendix:Proto-Indo-European/h₂éwsōs"), the ancestor of the Latin word _[aurora](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurora "Aurora")_'dawn'.[\[20\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-20) This etymological relationship is presumably behind the frequent claim in scientific publications that _aurum_ meant 'shining dawn'.[\[21\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Christie-2011-21)

## Characteristics

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Small_gold_nugget_5mm_dia_and_corresponding_foil_surface_of_half_sq_meter.jpg/250px-Small_gold_nugget_5mm_dia_and_corresponding_foil_surface_of_half_sq_meter.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Small_gold_nugget_5mm_dia_and_corresponding_foil_surface_of_half_sq_meter.jpg) A 5 mm (0.20 in) gold nugget (dot in front of frame) can be hammered into a [gold foil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_foil "Gold foil") of about 0.5 m2 (5.4 sq ft) in area.

Gold is the most [malleable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malleable "Malleable") metal. It can be drawn into a [wire of single-atom width](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanowire "Nanowire"), and then stretched considerably before it breaks.[\[22\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Kizuka-2008-22) Such nanowires distort via the formation, reorientation, and migration of [dislocations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dislocation "Dislocation") and [crystal twins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crystal_twinning "Crystal twinning") without noticeable hardening.[\[23\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-23) A single gram of gold can be beaten into a sheet of 1 square meter (11 sq ft), and an [avoirdupois ounce](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avoirdupois_ounce "Avoirdupois ounce") into 28 square meters (300 sq ft). Gold leaf can be beaten thin enough to become semi-transparent. Light transmitted through gold appears greenish-blue, because gold strongly reflects yellow and red.[\[24\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-24) Such semi-transparent sheets also strongly reflect [infrared](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared "Infrared") light, making them useful as infrared (radiant heat) shields in the visors of heat-resistant suits and in sun visors for [spacesuits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spacesuit "Spacesuit").[\[25\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-25) Gold is a good [conductor of heat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conduction_(heat) "Conduction (heat)") and [electricity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_conductor "Electrical conductor").

Gold has a density of 19.3 g/cm3, almost identical to that of [tungsten](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tungsten "Tungsten") at 19.25 g/cm3; as such, tungsten has been used in the [counterfeiting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Counterfeiting "Counterfeiting") of [gold bars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_bar "Gold bar"), such as by plating a tungsten bar with gold.[\[26\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Gray-2008-26)[\[27\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-27)[\[28\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-28)[\[29\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-29) By comparison, the density of [lead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lead "Lead") is 11.34 g/cm3, and that of the densest element, [osmium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osmium "Osmium"), is 22.588±0.015 g/cm3.[\[30\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Arblaster-1995-30)

### Color

Main article: [Colored gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colored_gold "Colored gold")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Gold_bullion_ap_001.JPG/250px-Gold_bullion_ap_001.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gold_bullion_ap_001.JPG) Gold bars, also called ingots or bullion[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Ag-Au-Cu-colours-english.svg/250px-Ag-Au-Cu-colours-english.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ag-Au-Cu-colours-english.svg) Different colors of [Ag](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silver "Silver")–Au– [Cu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copper "Copper") alloys

Whereas most metals are gray or silvery white, gold is slightly reddish-yellow.[\[31\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Lippincott-1880-31) This color is a well-known example of [relativistic quantum chemistry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relativistic_quantum_chemistry "Relativistic quantum chemistry"). The 5d-6s band gap is greatly reduced when relativity is included in theoretical calculations, and this is thought to account for the yellow color, although a full comparison of the absorption spectrum between the relativistic and non-relativistic cases has not been performed as of 2004.[\[32\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-32) Similar effects impart a golden hue to metallic [caesium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caesium "Caesium").

Common colored gold alloys include the distinctive eighteen-karat [rose gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rose_gold "Rose gold") created by the addition of copper. Alloys containing [palladium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palladium "Palladium") or [nickel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nickel "Nickel") are also important in commercial jewelry as these produce white gold alloys. Fourteen-karat gold-copper alloy is nearly identical in color to certain [bronze](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronze "Bronze") alloys, and both may be used to produce police and other [badges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Badge "Badge"). Fourteen- and eighteen-karat gold alloys with silver alone appear greenish-yellow and are referred to as [green gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_gold "Green gold"). Blue gold can be made by alloying with [iron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron "Iron"), and purple gold can be made by alloying with [aluminium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aluminium "Aluminium"). Less commonly, addition of [manganese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manganese "Manganese"), [indium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indium "Indium"), and other elements can produce more unusual colors of gold for various applications.[\[33\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-WorldGoldCouncil-33)

[Colloidal gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colloidal_gold "Colloidal gold"), used by electron-microscopists, is red if the particles are small; larger particles of colloidal gold are blue.[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-34)

### Isotopes

Main article: [Isotopes of gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotopes_of_gold "Isotopes of gold")

Gold has only one stable [isotope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotope "Isotope"), 197

Au, which is also its only naturally occurring isotope, so gold is both a [mononuclidic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mononuclidic_element "Mononuclidic element") and [monoisotopic element](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monoisotopic_element "Monoisotopic element"). Thirty-six [radioisotopes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radioisotopes "Radioisotopes") have been synthesized, ranging in [atomic mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_mass "Atomic mass") from 169 to 205. The most stable of these is 195

Au with a [half-life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Half-life "Half-life") of 186.1 days. The least stable is 171

Au, which decays by [proton emission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton_emission "Proton emission") with a half-life of 30 μs. Most of gold's radioisotopes with atomic masses below 197 decay by some combination of proton emission, [α decay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_decay "Alpha decay"), and [β+ decay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%92%2B_decay "Β+ decay"). The exceptions are 195

Au, which decays by electron capture, and 196

Au, which decays most often by electron capture (93%) with a minor [β− decay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%92%E2%88%92_decay "Β− decay") path (7%).[\[35\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-35) All of gold's radioisotopes with atomic masses above 197 decay by β− decay.[\[36\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Audi-2003-36)

At least 32 [nuclear isomers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_isomer "Nuclear isomer") have also been characterized, ranging in atomic mass from 170 to 200. Within that range, only 178

Au, 180

Au, 181

Au, 182

Au, and 188

Au do not have isomers. Gold's most stable isomer is 198m2

Au with a half-life of 2.27 days. Gold's least stable isomer is 177m2

Au with a half-life of only 7 ns. 184m1

Au has three decay paths: β+ decay, [isomeric transition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isomeric_transition "Isomeric transition"), and alpha decay. No other isomer or isotope of gold has three decay paths.[\[36\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Audi-2003-36)

#### Synthesis

See also: [Synthesis of precious metals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synthesis_of_precious_metals "Synthesis of precious metals")

The possible production of gold from a more common element, such as [lead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lead "Lead"), has long been a subject of human inquiry, and the ancient and medieval discipline of [alchemy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alchemy "Alchemy") often focused on it; however, the transmutation of the chemical elements did not become possible until the understanding of [nuclear physics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_physics "Nuclear physics") in the 20th century. The first synthesis of gold was conducted by Japanese physicist [Hantaro Nagaoka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hantaro_Nagaoka "Hantaro Nagaoka"), who synthesized gold from [mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(element) "Mercury (element)") in 1924 by neutron bombardment.[\[37\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-37) An American team, working without knowledge of Nagaoka's prior study, conducted the same experiment in 1941, achieving the same result and showing that the [isotopes of gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotopes_of_gold "Isotopes of gold") produced by it were all [radioactive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radioactive "Radioactive").[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-38) In 1980, [Glenn Seaborg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg "Glenn T. Seaborg") transmuted several thousand atoms of bismuth into gold at the Lawrence Berkeley Laboratory.[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-39)[\[40\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-40) Gold can be manufactured in a nuclear reactor, but doing so is highly impractical and would cost far more than the value of the gold that is produced.[\[41\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-41)

## Chemistry

Main article: [Gold compounds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_compounds "Gold compounds")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Gold%28III%29_chloride_solution.jpg/250px-Gold%28III%29_chloride_solution.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gold(III)_chloride_solution.jpg) Gold(III) chloride solution in water

Although gold is the most noble of the noble metals,[\[42\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-42)[\[43\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-43) it still forms many diverse compounds. The [oxidation state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxidation_state "Oxidation state") of gold in its compounds ranges from −1 to +5, but Au(I) and Au(III) dominate its chemistry. Au(I), referred to as the aurous ion, is the most common oxidation state with soft [ligands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ligand "Ligand") such as [thioethers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thioether "Thioether"), [thiolates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thiolate "Thiolate"), and [organophosphines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organophosphine "Organophosphine"). Au(I) compounds are typically linear. A good example is [Au(CN)−2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_cyanidation "Gold cyanidation"), which is the soluble form of gold encountered in mining. The binary [gold halides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_halide "Gold halide"), such as [AuCl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold(I)_chloride "Gold(I) chloride"), form zigzag polymeric chains, again featuring linear coordination at Au. Most [drugs based on gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold-containing_drugs "Gold-containing drugs") are Au(I) derivatives.[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-44)

Au(III) (referred to as auric) is a common oxidation state, and is illustrated by [gold(III) chloride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold(III)_chloride "Gold(III) chloride"), Au2Cl6. The gold atom centers in Au(III) complexes, like other d8 compounds, are typically [square planar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_planar "Square planar"), with [chemical bonds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_bond "Chemical bond") that have both [covalent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Covalent "Covalent") and [ionic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ion "Ion") character. [Gold(I,III) chloride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold(I,III)_chloride "Gold(I,III) chloride") is also known, an example of a [mixed-valence complex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mixed-valence_complex "Mixed-valence complex").

Gold does not react with oxygen at any temperature[\[45\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-45) and, up to 100 °C, is resistant to attack from ozone:[\[46\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-46)Au+O2⟶(no reaction){\\displaystyle {\\ce {Au + O2 ->}}({\\text{no reaction}})}![{\displaystyle {\mathrm {Au} {}+{}\mathrm {O} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{2}}{}\mathrel {\longrightarrow } {}}({\text{no reaction}})}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1042f66a95a991a8ce4c2e86c7748fd4afc327d4)Au+O3→t<100∘C(no reaction){\\displaystyle {\\ce {Au{}+O3->\[{\\mathit {t}}<100^{\\circ }{\\text{C}}\]}}({\\text{no reaction}})}![{\displaystyle {\mathrm {Au} {}+{}\mathrm {O} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{3}}{}\mathrel {\xrightarrow {{\mathit {t}}{<}100{\vphantom {A}}^{\circ }{\text{C}}} } {}}({\text{no reaction}})}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8769426b8bb7643281baf210ce9de23f96cbf3dd)

Some free [halogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halogen "Halogen") react to form the corresponding gold halides.[\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-47) Gold is strongly attacked by fluorine at dull-red heat[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-48) to form [gold(III) fluoride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold(III)_fluoride "Gold(III) fluoride") AuF3. Powdered gold reacts with chlorine at 180 °C to form [gold(III) chloride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold(III)_chloride "Gold(III) chloride") AuCl3.[\[49\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-49) Gold reacts with bromine at 140 °C to form a combination of [gold(III) bromide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold(III)_bromide "Gold(III) bromide") AuBr3 and [gold(I) bromide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold(I)_bromide "Gold(I) bromide") AuBr, but reacts very slowly with iodine to form [gold(I) iodide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold(I)_iodide "Gold(I) iodide") AuI:
2Au+3F2→Δ2AuF3{\\displaystyle {\\ce {2 Au{}+ 3 F2 ->\[\\Delta\] 2 AuF3}}}![{\displaystyle {2\,\mathrm {Au} {}+{}3\,\mathrm {F} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{2}}{}\mathrel {\xrightarrow {\mathrm {\Delta } } } {}2\,\mathrm {AuF} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{3}}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9a3c0bb0cc3c690e8240ab773bcb3da6c73da854)2Au+3Cl2→Δ2AuCl3{\\displaystyle {\\ce {2 Au{}+ 3 Cl2 ->\[\\Delta\] 2 AuCl3}}}![{\displaystyle {2\,\mathrm {Au} {}+{}3\,\mathrm {Cl} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{2}}{}\mathrel {\xrightarrow {\mathrm {\Delta } } } {}2\,\mathrm {AuCl} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{3}}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a7599e2064ec4dfd0bd0cee7a05f15d326d858cf)2Au+2Br2→ΔAuBr3+AuBr{\\displaystyle {\\ce {2 Au{}+ 2 Br2 ->\[\\Delta\] AuBr3{}+ AuBr}}}![{\displaystyle {2\,\mathrm {Au} {}+{}2\,\mathrm {Br} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{2}}{}\mathrel {\xrightarrow {\mathrm {\Delta } } } {}\mathrm {AuBr} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{3}}{}+{}\mathrm {AuBr} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6bbb59092bec2c4792f4b56103add365331527ed)2Au+I2→Δ2AuI{\\displaystyle {\\ce {2 Au{}+ I2 ->\[\\Delta\] 2 AuI}}}![{\displaystyle {2\,\mathrm {Au} {}+{}\mathrm {I} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{2}}{}\mathrel {\xrightarrow {\mathrm {\Delta } } } {}2\,\mathrm {AuI} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1d156530d3df908f9458571d0eeff185f7d647d6)

Gold does not react with sulfur directly,[\[50\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Emery-1961-50) but [gold(III) sulfide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold(III)_sulfide "Gold(III) sulfide") can be made by passing [hydrogen sulfide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide "Hydrogen sulfide") through a dilute solution of gold(III) chloride or [chlorauric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chlorauric_acid "Chlorauric acid").

Unlike sulfur, phosphorus reacts directly with gold at elevated temperatures to produce [gold phosphide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_phosphide "Gold phosphide") (Au2P3).[\[51\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-51)

Gold readily dissolves in [mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(element) "Mercury (element)") at room temperature to form an [amalgam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amalgam_(chemistry) "Amalgam (chemistry)"), and forms [alloys](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alloy "Alloy") with many other metals at higher temperatures. These alloys can be produced to modify the hardness and other metallurgical properties, to control [melting point](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melting_point "Melting point") or to create exotic colors.[\[33\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-WorldGoldCouncil-33)

Gold is unaffected by most acids. It does not react with [hydrofluoric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid "Hydrofluoric acid"), [hydrochloric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid "Hydrochloric acid"), [hydrobromic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrobromic_acid "Hydrobromic acid"), [hydriodic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydriodic_acid "Hydriodic acid"), [sulfuric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfuric_acid "Sulfuric acid"), or [nitric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitric_acid "Nitric acid"). It does react with [selenic acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selenic_acid "Selenic acid"), and is dissolved by [aqua regia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aqua_regia "Aqua regia"), a 1:3 mixture of nitric acid and hydrochloric acid. Nitric acid oxidizes the metal to +3 ions, but only in minute amounts, typically undetectable in the pure acid because of the chemical equilibrium of the reaction. However, the ions are removed from the equilibrium by hydrochloric acid, forming AuCl−4 ions, or [chloroauric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chloroauric_acid "Chloroauric acid"), thereby enabling further oxidation:
2Au+6H2SeO4→200∘CAu2(SeO4)3+3H2SeO3+3H2O{\\displaystyle {\\ce {2Au{}+6H2SeO4->\[200^{\\circ }{\\text{C}}\]Au2(SeO4)3{}+3H2SeO3{}+3H2O}}}![{\displaystyle {2\,\mathrm {Au} {}+{}6\,\mathrm {H} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{2}}\mathrm {SeO} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{4}}{}\mathrel {\xrightarrow {200{\vphantom {A}}^{\circ }{\text{C}}} } {}\mathrm {Au} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{2}}(\mathrm {SeO} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{4}}){\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{3}}{}+{}3\,\mathrm {H} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{2}}\mathrm {SeO} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{3}}{}+{}3\,\mathrm {H} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{2}}\mathrm {O} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/61712cffb00c6ce3259b513085089c5f5fd079b7)Au+4HCl+HNO3⟶HAuCl4+NO↑+2H2O{\\displaystyle {\\ce {Au{}+4HCl{}+HNO3->HAuCl4{}+NO\\uparrow +2H2O}}}![{\displaystyle {\mathrm {Au} {}+{}4\,\mathrm {HCl} {}+{}\mathrm {HNO} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{3}}{}\mathrel {\longrightarrow } {}\mathrm {HAuCl} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{4}}{}+{}\mathrm {NO} \uparrow {}+{}2\,\mathrm {H} {\vphantom {A}}_{\smash[{t}]{2}}\mathrm {O} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/927c524a984069045f198eed04c352d5263c6d5c)

Gold is similarly unaffected by most bases. It does not react with [aqueous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aqueous_solution "Aqueous solution"), [solid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid "Solid"), or [molten](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molten "Molten") [sodium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide "Sodium hydroxide") or [potassium hydroxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide "Potassium hydroxide"). It does, however, react with [sodium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium_cyanide "Sodium cyanide") or [potassium cyanide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potassium_cyanide "Potassium cyanide") under alkaline conditions when [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen") is present to form soluble complexes.[\[50\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Emery-1961-50)

Common [oxidation states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxidation_state "Oxidation state") of gold include +1 (gold(I) or aurous compounds) and +3 (gold(III) or auric compounds). Gold ions in solution are readily [reduced](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reduction_(chemistry) "Reduction (chemistry)") and [precipitated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry) "Precipitation (chemistry)") as metal by adding any other metal as the [reducing agent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reducing_agent "Reducing agent"). The added metal is [oxidized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxidation "Oxidation") and dissolves, allowing the gold to be displaced from solution and be recovered as a solid precipitate.

### Rare oxidation states

Less common oxidation states of gold include −1, +2, and +5.

The −1 oxidation state occurs in aurides, compounds containing the Au− [anion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anion "Anion"). [Caesium auride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caesium_auride "Caesium auride") (CsAu), for example, crystallizes in the [caesium chloride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caesium_chloride "Caesium chloride") motif;[\[52\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Jansen-2005-52) rubidium, potassium, and [tetramethylammonium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetramethylammonium "Tetramethylammonium") aurides are also known.[\[53\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Holleman-2001-53) Gold has the highest [electron affinity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_affinity "Electron affinity") of any metal, at 222.8 kJ/mol, making Au− a stable species,[\[54\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Jansen-2008-54) analogous to the [halides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halide "Halide").

Gold also has a –1 oxidation state in covalent complexes with the [group 4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Group_4_element "Group 4 element") transition metals, such as in titanium tetraauride and the analogous zirconium and hafnium compounds. These chemicals are expected to form gold-bridged [dimers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry) "Dimer (chemistry)") in a manner similar to [titanium(IV) hydride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titanium(IV)_hydride "Titanium(IV) hydride").[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-55)

Gold(II) compounds are usually [diamagnetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diamagnetic "Diamagnetic") with Au–Au bonds such as \[Au(CH2)2P(C6H5)2\]2Cl2. The evaporation of a solution of Au(OH)3 in concentrated H2SO4 produces red crystals of [gold(II) sulfate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold(II)_sulfate "Gold(II) sulfate"), Au2(SO4)2. Originally thought to be a mixed-valence compound, it has been shown to contain Au4+2 cations, analogous to the better-known [mercury(I)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury(I) "Mercury(I)") ion, Hg2+2.[\[56\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-56)[\[57\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-57) A gold(II) complex, the [tetraxenonogold(II)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetraxenonogold(II) "Tetraxenonogold(II)") cation, which contains [xenon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xenon "Xenon") as a ligand, occurs in \[AuXe4\](Sb2F11)2.[\[58\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-58) In September 2023, a novel type of [metal-halide perovskite material](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perovskite_(structure) "Perovskite (structure)") consisting of Au3+ and Au2+ cations in its crystal structure has been found.[\[59\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-59) It has been shown to be unexpectedly stable at normal conditions.

[Gold pentafluoride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_pentafluoride "Gold pentafluoride"), along with its derivative anion, AuF−6, and its [difluorine complex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Difluorine_complex "Difluorine complex"), [gold heptafluoride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_heptafluoride "Gold heptafluoride"), is the sole example of gold(V), the highest verified oxidation state.[\[60\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-60)

Some gold compounds exhibit _[aurophilic bonding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurophilicity "Aurophilicity")_, which describes the tendency of gold ions to interact at distances that are too long to be a conventional Au–Au bond but shorter than [van der Waals bonding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force "Van der Waals force"). The interaction is estimated to be comparable in strength to that of a [hydrogen bond](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_bond "Hydrogen bond").

Well-defined cluster compounds are numerous.[\[53\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Holleman-2001-53) In some cases, gold has a fractional oxidation state. A representative example is the octahedral species {Au( [P(C6H5)3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triphenylphosphine "Triphenylphosphine"))}2+6.

## Origin

### Gold production in the universe

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Vredefort_crater_cross_section_2.png/500px-Vredefort_crater_cross_section_2.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Vredefort_crater_cross_section_2.png) Schematic of a NE (left) to SW (right) cross-section through the 2.020-billion-year-old [Vredefort impact structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vredefort_impact_structure "Vredefort impact structure") in [South Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Africa "South Africa") and how it distorted the contemporary geological structures. The present erosion level is shown. [Johannesburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johannesburg "Johannesburg") is located where the [Witwatersrand Basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witwatersrand_Basin "Witwatersrand Basin") (the yellow layer) is exposed at the "present surface" line, just inside the crater rim, on the left. Not to scale.

Gold in the universe is produced through several cosmic processes and was present in the [dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_nebula "Solar nebula") from which the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System") formed.[\[61\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-61) Scientists have identified three main cosmic sources for gold formation: [supernova nucleosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis "Supernova nucleosynthesis"), [neutron star collisions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron_star_merger "Neutron star merger"),[\[62\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-62) and magnetar flares.

All three sources involve a process called the [r-process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R-process "R-process") (rapid neutron capture), which forms elements heavier than [iron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron "Iron").[\[63\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-63) For decades, scientists believed [supernova nucleosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis "Supernova nucleosynthesis") was the primary mechanism for gold formation. More recently, research has shown that [neutron star collisions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron_star_merger "Neutron star merger") produce significant quantities of gold through the r-process.[\[64\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-64)

In August 2017, the spectroscopic signatures of heavy elements, including gold, were directly observed by electromagnetic observatories during the [GW170817](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GW170817 "GW170817") neutron star merger event.[\[65\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-65) This confirmed neutron star mergers as a source of gold, after years of only indirect detection.[\[66\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-66) This single event generated between 3 and 13 [Earth masses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_mass "Earth mass") of gold, suggesting that neutron star mergers might produce enough gold to account for most of this element in the universe.[\[67\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-67)

However, neutron star mergers alone cannot explain all cosmic gold, particularly in older stars, because these mergers occur relatively late in galactic history and are infrequent (approximately once every 100,000 years).[\[68\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-68) This created a timing paradox in explaining the presence of gold in stars formed early in the universe.

In 2025, researchers resolved this paradox by confirming that giant flares from [magnetars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetar "Magnetar") (highly magnetic neutron stars) are also a significant source of gold formation.[\[69\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-69) Analysis of a 2004 magnetar flare showed these events produce heavy elements through the same r-process as neutron star mergers. The amount of heavy elements created in a single magnetar flare can exceed the mass of Mars.[\[70\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-70) Since magnetars existed earlier in cosmic history and flare more frequently than neutron star mergers occur, they help explain gold's presence in older stars. Scientists estimate magnetar flares may contribute approximately 1–10% of all elements heavier than iron in our galaxy, including gold.[\[71\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-71)

### Asteroid origin theories

Because the Earth was molten [when it was formed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Earth "History of Earth"), almost all of the gold present in the [early Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Earth "Early Earth") probably sank into the [planetary core](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Core_(geology) "Core (geology)"). Therefore, as hypothesized in one model, most of the gold in the Earth's [crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crust_(geology) "Crust (geology)") and [mantle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mantle_(geology) "Mantle (geology)") is thought to have been delivered to Earth by [asteroid impacts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid_impact "Asteroid impact") during the [Late Heavy Bombardment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment "Late Heavy Bombardment"), about 4 billion years ago.[\[72\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Willbold-2011-72)[\[73\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Battison-2011-73)

Gold which is reachable by humans has, in one case, been associated with a particular asteroid impact. The asteroid that formed [Vredefort impact structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vredefort_impact_structure "Vredefort impact structure") 2.020 billion years ago is often credited with seeding the [Witwatersrand basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witwatersrand_basin "Witwatersrand basin") in [South Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Africa "South Africa") with the richest gold deposits on earth.[\[74\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-74)[\[75\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-75)[\[76\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-76)[\[77\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-77) However, this scenario is now questioned. The gold-bearing [Witwatersrand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witwatersrand "Witwatersrand") rocks were laid down between 700 and 950 million years before the Vredefort impact.[\[78\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-McCarthy-2005-78)[\[79\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Norman-2006-79) These gold-bearing rocks had furthermore been covered by a thick layer of Ventersdorp lavas and the [Transvaal Supergroup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transvaal_Basin "Transvaal Basin") of rocks before the meteor struck, and thus the gold did not actually arrive in the asteroid/meteorite. What the Vredefort impact achieved, however, was to distort the Witwatersrand basin in such a way that the gold-bearing rocks were brought to the present [erosion surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erosion_surface "Erosion surface") in [Johannesburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johannesburg "Johannesburg"), on the Witwatersrand, just inside the rim of the original 300 km (190 mi) diameter crater caused by the meteor strike. The discovery of the deposit in 1886 launched the [Witwatersrand Gold Rush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witwatersrand_Gold_Rush "Witwatersrand Gold Rush"). Some 22% of all the gold that is ascertained to exist today on Earth has been extracted from these Witwatersrand rocks.[\[79\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Norman-2006-79)

### Mantle return theories

Much of the rest of the gold on Earth is thought to have been incorporated into the planet since its very beginning, as [planetesimals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetesimals "Planetesimals") formed the [mantle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mantle_(geology) "Mantle (geology)"). In 2017, an international group of scientists established that gold "came to the Earth's surface from the deepest regions of our planet",[\[80\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-80) the mantle, as evidenced by their findings at [Deseado Massif](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deseado_Massif "Deseado Massif") in the [Argentinian Patagonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argentinian_Patagonia "Argentinian Patagonia").[\[81\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-81)\[ _[clarification needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify "Wikipedia:Please clarify")_\]

## Occurrence

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Gold_nugget_%28Australia%29_4_%2816848647509%29.jpg/250px-Gold_nugget_%28Australia%29_4_%2816848647509%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gold_nugget_(Australia)_4_(16848647509).jpg) [Native gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Native_metal "Native metal")

On Earth, gold is found in [ores](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ore "Ore") in rock formed from the [Precambrian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precambrian "Precambrian") time onward.[\[82\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-La_Niece-2009-82) It most often occurs as a [native metal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Native_metal "Native metal"), typically in a metal [solid solution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid_solution "Solid solution") with silver (i.e. as a gold/silver [alloy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alloy "Alloy")). Such alloys usually have a silver content of 8–10%. [Electrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrum "Electrum") is elemental gold with more than 20% silver, and is commonly known as [white gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_gold "White gold"). Electrum's color runs from golden-silvery to silvery, dependent upon the silver content. The more silver, the lower the [specific gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Specific_gravity "Specific gravity").

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Gold-Pyrite-263192.jpg/250px-Gold-Pyrite-263192.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gold-Pyrite-263192.jpg) Gold in pyrite

Native gold occurs as very small to microscopic particles embedded in rock, often together with [quartz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quartz "Quartz") or [sulfide minerals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfide_mineral "Sulfide mineral") such as " [fool's gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fool%27s_gold "Fool's gold")", which is a [pyrite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrite "Pyrite").[\[83\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-83) These are called [lode](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lode "Lode") deposits. The metal in a native state is also found in the form of free flakes, grains or larger [nuggets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_nugget "Gold nugget")[\[82\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-La_Niece-2009-82) that have been eroded from rocks and end up in [alluvial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alluvial "Alluvial") deposits called [placer deposits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Placer_deposit "Placer deposit"). Such free gold is always richer at the exposed surface of gold-bearing veins, owing to the [oxidation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxidation "Oxidation") of accompanying minerals followed by weathering; and by washing of the dust into streams and rivers, where it collects and can be welded by water action to form nuggets.

Gold sometimes occurs combined with [tellurium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tellurium "Tellurium") as the [minerals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mineral "Mineral") [calaverite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calaverite "Calaverite"), [krennerite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Krennerite "Krennerite"), [nagyagite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nagyagite "Nagyagite"), [petzite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petzite "Petzite") and [sylvanite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sylvanite "Sylvanite") (see [telluride minerals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telluride_mineral "Telluride mineral")), and as the rare bismuthide maldonite (Au2Bi) and antimonide [aurostibite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurostibite "Aurostibite") (AuSb2). Gold also occurs in rare alloys with [copper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copper "Copper"), [lead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lead "Lead"), and [mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(element) "Mercury (element)"): the minerals [auricupride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auricupride "Auricupride") (Cu3Au), novodneprite (AuPb3) and weishanite ((Au,Ag)3Hg2).

A 2004 research paper suggests that microbes can sometimes play an important role in forming gold deposits, transporting and precipitating gold to form grains and nuggets that collect in alluvial deposits.[\[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-84)

A 2013 study has claimed water in faults vaporizes during an earthquake, depositing gold. When an earthquake strikes, it moves along a [fault](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fault_(geology) "Fault (geology)"). Water often lubricates faults, filling in fractures and jogs. About 10 kilometers (6.2 mi) below the surface, under very high temperatures and pressures, the water carries high concentrations of carbon dioxide, silica, and gold. During an earthquake, the fault jog suddenly opens wider. The water inside the void instantly vaporizes, flashing to steam and forcing silica, which forms the mineral quartz, and gold out of the fluids and onto nearby surfaces.[\[85\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-85)

### Seawater

The world's [oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean "Ocean") contain gold. Measured concentrations of gold in the Atlantic and Northeast Pacific are 50–150 [femtomol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Femtomolar "Femtomolar")/L or 10–30 parts per [quadrillion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadrillion "Quadrillion") (about 10–30 g/km3). In general, gold concentrations for south Atlantic and central Pacific samples are the same (~50 femtomol/L) but less certain. Mediterranean deep waters contain slightly higher concentrations of gold (100–150 femtomol/L), which is attributed to wind-blown dust or rivers. At 10 parts per quadrillion, the Earth's [oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oceans "Oceans") would hold 15,000 tonnes of gold.[\[86\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-86) These figures are three orders of magnitude less than reported in the literature prior to 1988, indicating contamination problems with the earlier data.

A number of people have claimed to be able to economically recover gold from [sea water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_water "Sea water"), but they were either mistaken or acted in an intentional deception. [Prescott Jernegan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prescott_Jernegan "Prescott Jernegan") ran a gold-from-seawater swindle in the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States") in the 1890s, as did an English fraudster in the early 1900s.[\[87\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-87) [Fritz Haber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fritz_Haber "Fritz Haber") did research on the extraction of gold from sea water in an effort to help pay [Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germany "Germany")'s reparations following [World War I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_I "World War I").[\[88\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-88) Based on the published values of 2 to 64 ppb of gold in seawater, a commercially successful extraction seemed possible. After analysis of 4,000 water samples yielding an average of 0.004 ppb, it became clear that extraction would not be possible, and he ended the project.[\[89\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-89)

## History

Main article: [History of gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_gold "History of gold")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Indian_gold_tribute_donor_Apadana.jpg/250px-Indian_gold_tribute_donor_Apadana.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Indian_gold_tribute_donor_Apadana.jpg) An Indian tribute-bearer at [Apadana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apadana "Apadana"), from the [Achaemenid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire "Achaemenid Empire") [satrapy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satrapy "Satrapy") of _[Hindush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindush "Hindush")_, carrying gold on a yoke, circa 500 BC.[\[90\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Iran-1972-90)

The earliest recorded metal employed by humans appears to be gold. Small amounts of natural gold have been found in Spanish caves used during the late [Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paleolithic "Paleolithic") period, c. 40,000 BC.[\[91\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-91)

The oldest gold artifacts in the world are from [Bulgaria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulgaria "Bulgaria") and are dating back to the 5th millennium BC, such as those found in the [Varna Necropolis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Varna_Necropolis "Varna Necropolis") near Lake Varna and the [Black Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Sea "Black Sea") coast, thought to be the earliest "well-dated" finding of gold artifacts in history.[\[92\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-92)[\[82\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-La_Niece-2009-82)[\[93\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-93)

Gold artifacts probably made their appearance in Ancient Egypt at the beginning of the pre-dynastic period, at the end of the fifth millennium BC and the start of the fourth, and smelting was developed during the course of the 4th millennium; gold artifacts appear in the archeology of Lower Mesopotamia during the early 4th millennium.[\[94\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-94) As of 1990, gold artifacts found at the [Wadi Qana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wadi_Qana "Wadi Qana") cave cemetery of the [4th millennium BC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/4th_millennium_BC "4th millennium BC") in [West Bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Bank "West Bank") were the earliest from the Levant.[\[95\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Gopher-1990-95) Gold artifacts such as the [golden hats](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_hats "Golden hats") and the [Nebra disk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nebra_disk "Nebra disk") appeared in Central Europe from the 2nd millennium BC [Bronze Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Bronze_Age "European Bronze Age").

Exploitation of gold in the south-east corner of the [Black Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Sea "Black Sea") is said to date from the time of [Midas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Midas "Midas"), and this gold was important in the establishment of what is probably the world's earliest coinage in [Lydia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lydia "Lydia") around 610 BC.[\[96\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Lion-2003-96)

During [Mansa Musa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mansa_Musa "Mansa Musa")'s (ruler of the [Mali Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mali_Empire "Mali Empire") from 1312 to 1337) [hajj](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hajj "Hajj") to [Mecca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mecca "Mecca") in 1324, he passed through [Cairo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cairo "Cairo") in July 1324, and was reportedly accompanied by a [camel train](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Camel_train "Camel train") that included thousands of people and nearly a hundred camels where he gave away so much gold that it depressed the price in Egypt for over a decade, causing high [inflation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inflation "Inflation").[\[97\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-97)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Monnaie_de_Bactriane%2C_Eucratide_I%2C_2_faces.jpg/250px-Monnaie_de_Bactriane%2C_Eucratide_I%2C_2_faces.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Monnaie_de_Bactriane,_Eucratide_I,_2_faces.jpg) Gold coin of [Eucratides I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eucratides_I "Eucratides I") (171–145 BC), one of the Hellenistic rulers of ancient [Ai-Khanoum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ai-Khanoum "Ai-Khanoum"). This is the largest known gold coin minted in antiquity (169.2 g (5.97 oz); 58 mm (2.3 in)).[\[98\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-98)

The European exploration of the Americas was fueled in no small part by reports of the gold ornaments displayed in great profusion by [Native American](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas "Indigenous peoples of the Americas") peoples, especially in [Mesoamerica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesoamerica "Mesoamerica"), [Peru](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peru "Peru"), [Ecuador](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecuador "Ecuador") and [Colombia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colombia "Colombia"). The [Aztecs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aztec "Aztec") regarded gold as the product of the gods, calling it literally "god excrement" ( _teocuitlatl_ in [Nahuatl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nahuatl "Nahuatl")), and after [Moctezuma II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moctezuma_II "Moctezuma II") was killed, most of this gold was shipped to Spain.[\[99\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-99) However, for the [indigenous peoples of North America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_North_America "Indigenous peoples of North America") gold was considered useless and they saw much greater value in other [minerals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minerals "Minerals") which were directly related to their utility, such as [obsidian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Obsidian "Obsidian"), [flint](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flint "Flint"), and [slate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slate "Slate").[\[100\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-100)

Beginning in the [early modern period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_modern_period "Early modern period"), European [exploration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_Discovery "Age of Discovery") and [colonization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonisation_of_Africa "Colonisation of Africa") of [West Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Africa "West Africa") was driven in large part by reports of gold deposits in the region, which was eventually referred to by Europeans as the " [Gold Coast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_Coast_(region) "Gold Coast (region)")".[\[101\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-101) From the late 15th to early 19th centuries, European trade in the region was primarily focused in gold, along with [ivory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ivory "Ivory") and [slaves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade "Atlantic slave trade").[\[102\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-102) The gold trade in West Africa was dominated by the [Ashanti Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ashanti_Empire "Ashanti Empire"), who initially traded with the [Portuguese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portuguese_Empire "Portuguese Empire") before branching out and trading with [British](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Empire "British Empire"), [French](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_colonial_empire "French colonial empire"), [Spanish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_Empire "Spanish Empire") and [Danish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Danish_colonial_empire "Danish colonial empire") merchants.[\[103\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Green-2019-103) British desires to secure control of West African gold deposits played a role in the [Anglo-Ashanti wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Ashanti_wars "Anglo-Ashanti wars") of the late 19th century, which saw the Ashanti Empire [annexed by Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_Coast_(British_colony) "Gold Coast (British colony)").[\[104\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-104)

One main goal of the [alchemists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alchemy "Alchemy") was to produce gold from other substances, such as [lead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lead "Lead") — presumably by the interaction with a mythical substance called the [philosopher's stone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosopher%27s_stone "Philosopher's stone"). Trying to produce gold led the alchemists to systematically find out what can be done with substances, and this laid the foundation for today's [chemistry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemistry "Chemistry"), which can produce gold (albeit uneconomically) by using [nuclear transmutation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation "Nuclear transmutation").[\[105\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-105)

## Production

Main article: [List of countries by gold production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_gold_production "List of countries by gold production")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Gold_-_world_production_trend.svg/250px-Gold_-_world_production_trend.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gold_-_world_production_trend.svg%3Flang%3Den "File:Gold - world production trend.svg?lang=en") Time trend of gold production

According to the [United States Geological Survey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey "United States Geological Survey") in 2016, about 5,726,000,000 troy ounces (178,100 t) of gold has been accounted for, of which 85% remains in active use.[\[106\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-106)

### Mining and prospecting

Main articles: [Gold mining](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_mining "Gold mining") and [Gold prospecting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_prospecting "Gold prospecting")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Miner_underground_at_Pumsaint_gold_mine_%281294028%29.jpg/250px-Miner_underground_at_Pumsaint_gold_mine_%281294028%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Miner_underground_at_Pumsaint_gold_mine_(1294028).jpg) A miner underground at [Pumsaint](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pumsaint "Pumsaint") gold mine, [Wales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wales "Wales"); c. 1938.[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Grasberg_mine.jpg/250px-Grasberg_mine.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Grasberg_mine.jpg) [Grasberg mine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grasberg_mine "Grasberg mine"), in Indonesia, is the world's largest gold mine.

Since the 1880s, South Africa has been the source of a large proportion of the world's gold supply, and about 22% of the gold presently accounted is from South Africa. Production in 1970 accounted for 79% of the world supply, about 1,480 tonnes. In 2007 China (with 276 tonnes) overtook South Africa as the world's largest gold producer, the first time since 1905 that South Africa had not been the largest.[\[107\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-107)

In 2023, [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_mining_in_China "Gold mining in China") was the world's leading gold-mining country, followed in order by Russia, Australia, Canada, the United States and Ghana.[\[14\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Gold_Production-2023-14)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Gold_30g_for_a_860kg_rock.jpg/250px-Gold_30g_for_a_860kg_rock.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gold_30g_for_a_860kg_rock.jpg) Relative sizes of an 860 kg (1,900 lb) block of gold ore and the 30 g (0.96 ozt) of gold that can be extracted from it, [Toi gold mine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toi_gold_mine "Toi gold mine"), [Japan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japan "Japan").

In South America, the controversial project [Pascua Lama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascua_Lama "Pascua Lama") aims at exploitation of rich fields in the high mountains of [Atacama Desert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atacama_Desert "Atacama Desert"), at the border between [Chile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chile "Chile") and [Argentina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argentina "Argentina").

It has been estimated that up to one-quarter of the yearly global gold production originates from artisanal or small scale mining.[\[108\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-108)[\[109\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-109)[\[110\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-110)

The city of [Johannesburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johannesburg "Johannesburg") located in South Africa was founded as a result of the [Witwatersrand Gold Rush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witwatersrand_Gold_Rush "Witwatersrand Gold Rush") which resulted in the discovery of some of the largest natural gold deposits in recorded history. The gold fields are confined to the northern and north-western edges of the [Witwatersrand basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witwatersrand_basin "Witwatersrand basin"), which is a 5–7 km (3.1–4.3 mi) thick layer of [archean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archean "Archean") rocks located, in most places, deep under the [Free State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_State_(South_African_province) "Free State (South African province)"), [Gauteng](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauteng "Gauteng") and surrounding provinces.[\[111\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Truswell-1977-111) These Witwatersrand rocks are exposed at the surface on the [Witwatersrand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witwatersrand "Witwatersrand"), in and around Johannesburg, but also in isolated patches to the south-east and south-west of Johannesburg, as well as in an arc around the [Vredefort Dome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vredefort_Dome "Vredefort Dome") which lies close to the center of the Witwatersrand basin.[\[78\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-McCarthy-2005-78)[\[111\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Truswell-1977-111) From these surface exposures the basin [dips](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strike_and_dip "Strike and dip") extensively, requiring some of the mining to occur at depths of nearly 4,000 m (13,000 ft), making them, especially the [Savuka](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Savuka_Mine&action=edit&redlink=1 "Savuka Mine (page does not exist)") and [TauTona](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TauTona "TauTona") mines to the south-west of Johannesburg, the deepest mines on Earth. The gold is found only in six areas where archean rivers from the north and north-west formed extensive pebbly [Braided river](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Braided_river "Braided river") deltas before draining into the "Witwatersrand sea" where the rest of the Witwatersrand sediments were deposited.[\[111\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Truswell-1977-111)

The [Second Boer War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Boer_War "Second Boer War") of 1899–1901 between the [British Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Empire "British Empire") and the [Afrikaner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afrikaner "Afrikaner") [Boers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boer "Boer") was at least partly over the rights of miners and possession of the gold wealth in South Africa.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Kullanhuuhdontaa_Ivalossa.jpg/250px-Kullanhuuhdontaa_Ivalossa.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kullanhuuhdontaa_Ivalossa.jpg) Gold prospecting at the [Ivalo River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ivalo_River "Ivalo River") in the [Finnish Lapland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lapland_(Finland) "Lapland (Finland)") in 1898

During the 19th century, [gold rushes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_rush "Gold rush") occurred whenever large gold deposits were discovered. The first documented discovery of gold in the United States was at the [Reed Gold Mine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reed_Gold_Mine "Reed Gold Mine") near Georgeville, North Carolina in 1803.[\[112\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-112) The first major gold strike in the United States occurred in a small north Georgia town called [Dahlonega](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dahlonega,_Georgia "Dahlonega, Georgia").[\[113\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-113) Further gold rushes occurred in [California](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/California_Gold_Rush "California Gold Rush"), [Colorado](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pike%27s_Peak_Gold_Rush "Pike's Peak Gold Rush"), the [Black Hills](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Hills_Gold_Rush "Black Hills Gold Rush"), [Otago](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Otago_gold_rush "Otago gold rush") in New Zealand, a number of locations across [Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australian_gold_rushes "Australian gold rushes"), [Witwatersrand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witwatersrand_Gold_Rush "Witwatersrand Gold Rush") in South Africa, and the [Klondike](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Klondike_Gold_Rush "Klondike Gold Rush") in Canada.

[Grasberg mine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grasberg_mine "Grasberg mine") located in [Papua](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Papua_(province) "Papua (province)"), [Indonesia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indonesia "Indonesia") is the largest [gold mine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_mining "Gold mining") in the world.[\[114\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-114)

### Extraction and refining

Main article: [Gold extraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_extraction "Gold extraction")

[Gold extraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_extraction "Gold extraction") is most economical in large, easily mined deposits. Ore grades as little as 0.5 parts per million (ppm) can be economical. Typical ore grades in [open-pit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Open-pit_mining "Open-pit mining") mines are 1–5 ppm; ore grades in underground or [hard rock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Underground_mining_(hard_rock) "Underground mining (hard rock)") mines are usually at least 3 ppm. Because ore grades of 30 ppm are usually needed before gold is visible to the naked eye, in most gold mines the gold is invisible.

The average gold mining and extraction costs were about $317 per troy ounce in 2007 (equivalent to $492 in 2025), but these can vary widely depending on mining type and ore quality; global mine production amounted to 2,471.1 tonnes.[\[115\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-115)

After initial production, gold is often subsequently refined industrially by the [Wohlwill process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wohlwill_process "Wohlwill process") which is based on [electrolysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrolysis "Electrolysis") or by the [Miller process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miller_process "Miller process"), that is chlorination in the melt. The Wohlwill process results in higher purity, but is more complex and is only applied in small-scale installations.[\[116\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-116)[\[117\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-117) Other methods of assaying and purifying smaller amounts of gold include parting and inquartation as well as [cupellation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cupellation "Cupellation"), or refining methods based on the dissolution of gold in aqua regia.[\[118\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-118)

### Recycling

In 1997, recycled gold accounted for approximately 20% of the 2700 tons of gold supplied to the market.[\[119\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-119) Jewelry companies such as Generation Collection and computer companies including [Dell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dell "Dell") conduct recycling.[\[120\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-120)

As of 2020, the amount of [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide") CO2 produced in mining a kilogram of gold is 16 tonnes, while recycling a kilogram of gold produces 53 kilograms of CO2 equivalent. Approximately 30 percent of the global gold supply is recycled and not mined as of 2020.[\[121\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-121)
