### Pollution

Further information: [Mercury cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_cycle "Mercury cycle") and [International Cyanide Management Code](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Cyanide_Management_Code "International Cyanide Management Code")

Gold production is associated with contribution to hazardous [pollution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmental_effects_of_mining "Environmental effects of mining").[\[124\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-124)

Low-grade gold ore may contain less than one [ppm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parts_per_million "Parts per million") gold metal; such ore is [ground](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milling_(grinding) "Milling (grinding)") and mixed with [sodium cyanide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium_cyanide "Sodium cyanide") to dissolve the gold. Cyanide is a highly poisonous chemical, which can kill living creatures when exposed in minute quantities. Many [cyanide spills](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_gold_mining_disasters "List of gold mining disasters")[\[125\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-125) from gold mines have occurred in both developed and developing countries which killed aquatic life in long stretches of affected rivers. Environmentalists consider these events major environmental disasters.[\[126\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-126)[\[127\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-127) Up to thirty tons of used ore can be dumped as waste for producing one troy ounce of gold.[\[128\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-NYT-2005-128) Gold ore dumps are the source of many heavy elements such as cadmium, lead, zinc, copper, [arsenic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arsenic "Arsenic"), [selenium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selenium "Selenium") and mercury. When sulfide-bearing minerals in these ore dumps are exposed to air and water, the sulfide transforms into [sulfuric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfuric_acid "Sulfuric acid") which in turn dissolves these heavy metals facilitating their passage into surface water and ground water. This process is called [acid mine drainage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acid_mine_drainage "Acid mine drainage"). These gold ore dumps contain long-term, highly [hazardous waste](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hazardous_waste "Hazardous waste").[\[128\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-NYT-2005-128)

It was once common to use mercury to recover gold from ore, but today the use of mercury is largely limited to small-scale individual miners.[\[129\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-129) Minute quantities of mercury compounds can reach water bodies, causing heavy metal contamination. Mercury can then enter into the human food chain in the form of [methylmercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methylmercury "Methylmercury"). [Mercury poisoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_poisoning "Mercury poisoning") in humans can cause severe brain damage.[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-130)

Gold extraction is also a highly energy-intensive industry — extracting ore from deep mines and grinding the large quantity of ore for further chemical extraction requires nearly 25 [kWh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilowatt-hour "Kilowatt-hour") of electricity per gram of gold produced.[\[131\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-131)

## Monetary use

Further information: [History of money](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_money "History of money")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Two_20kr_gold_coins.png/250px-Two_20kr_gold_coins.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Two_20kr_gold_coins.png) Two golden 20 kr coins from the [Scandinavian Monetary Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scandinavian_Monetary_Union "Scandinavian Monetary Union"), which was based on a [gold standard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_standard "Gold standard"). The coin to the left is [Swedish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sweden "Sweden") and the right one is [Danish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Denmark "Denmark").

Gold has been [widely used](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_money "History of money") throughout the world as [money](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Money "Money"),[\[132\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-132) for efficient indirect exchange (versus [barter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barter "Barter")), and to store wealth in [hoards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hoard "Hoard"). For exchange purposes, [mints](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mint_(coin) "Mint (coin)") produce standardized [gold bullion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bullion "Bullion") [coins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coins "Coins"), [bars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_bar "Gold bar") and [other units](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Good_delivery "Good delivery") of fixed weight and purity.

The first known coins containing gold were struck in Lydia, Asia Minor, around 600 BC.[\[96\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Lion-2003-96) The _[talent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Talent_(measurement) "Talent (measurement)")_ coin of gold in use during the periods of Grecian history both before and during the time of the life of Homer weighed between 8.42 and 8.75 grams.[\[133\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-133) From an earlier preference in using silver, European economies re-established the minting of gold as coinage during the thirteenth and fourteenth centuries.[\[134\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Postan-1967-134)

[Bills](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_bills_doctrine "Real bills doctrine") (that mature into gold coin) and [gold certificates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_certificates "Gold certificates") (convertible into gold coin at the issuing bank) added to the circulating stock of [gold standard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_standard "Gold standard") money in most 19th century industrial economies. In preparation for [World War I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_I "World War I") the warring nations moved to fractional gold standards, inflating their currencies to finance the war effort. Post-war, the victorious countries, most notably Britain, gradually restored gold-convertibility, but international flows of gold via bills of exchange remained embargoed; international shipments were made exclusively for bilateral trades or to pay war reparations.

After [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II "World War II") gold was replaced by a system of nominally [convertible currencies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convertible_currency "Convertible currency") related by fixed exchange rates following the [Bretton Woods system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bretton_Woods_system "Bretton Woods system"). [Gold standards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_standard "Gold standard") and the direct convertibility of currencies to gold have been abandoned by world governments, led in 1971 by the United States' refusal to redeem its dollars in gold. [Fiat currency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiat_currency "Fiat currency") now fills most monetary roles. [Switzerland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Switzerland "Switzerland") was the last country to tie its currency to gold; this was ended by a referendum in 1999.[\[135\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-135)

Central banks continue to keep a portion of their liquid reserves as gold in some form, and metals exchanges such as the [London Bullion Market Association](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/London_Bullion_Market_Association "London Bullion Market Association") still clear transactions denominated in gold, including future delivery contracts. Today, [gold mining](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_mining "Gold mining") output is declining.[\[136\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-136) With the sharp growth of economies in the 20th century, and increasing foreign exchange, the world's [gold reserves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_reserve "Gold reserve") and their trading market have become a small fraction of all markets and fixed exchange rates of currencies to gold have been replaced by floating prices for gold and gold [future contract](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Futures_contract "Futures contract"). Though the gold stock grows by only 1% or 2% per year, very little metal is irretrievably consumed. Inventory above ground would satisfy many decades of industrial and even artisan uses at current prices.

The gold proportion ( [fineness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fineness "Fineness")) of alloys is measured by [karat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fineness#karat "Fineness") (k). Pure gold (commercially termed _fine_ gold) is designated as 24 karat, abbreviated 24k. English gold coins intended for circulation from 1526 into the 1930s were typically a standard 22k alloy called [crown gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crown_gold "Crown gold"),[\[137\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-137) for hardness (American gold coins for circulation after 1837 contain an alloy of 0.900 fine gold, or 21.6 kt).[\[138\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-138)

Often the prices of various [platinum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platinum "Platinum") group metals can be much higher than gold, although gold has been used as a standard for [currencies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Currency "Currency") to a greater degree than the platinum group metals. Gold has been used as a symbol for purity, value, royalty, and particularly roles that combine these properties. Gold as a sign of wealth and prestige was ridiculed by [Thomas More](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_More "Thomas More") in his treatise _[Utopia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utopia_(More_book) "Utopia (More book)")_. On that imaginary island, gold is so abundant that it is used to make chains for slaves, tableware, and lavatory seats. When ambassadors from other countries arrive, dressed in ostentatious gold jewels and badges, the Utopians mistake them for menial servants, paying homage instead to the most modestly dressed of their party.

The [ISO 4217](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISO_4217 "ISO 4217") currency code of gold is XAU.[\[139\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-139) Many holders of gold store it in form of [bullion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bullion "Bullion") coins or [bars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_bar "Gold bar") as a hedge against [inflation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inflation "Inflation") or other economic disruptions. A paper by the [National Bureau of Economic Research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Bureau_of_Economic_Research "National Bureau of Economic Research") found that gold may be reliable as an inflation hedge over long timescales (centuries) but not over practical timescales.[\[140\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-140) Modern [bullion coins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bullion_coin "Bullion coin") for investment or collector purposes do not require good mechanical wear properties; they are typically fine gold at 24k, although the [American Gold Eagle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Gold_Eagle "American Gold Eagle") and the British [gold sovereign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sovereign_(British_coin) "Sovereign (British coin)") continue to be minted in 22k (0.92) metal in historical tradition, and the South African [Krugerrand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Krugerrand "Krugerrand"), first released in 1967, is also 22k (0.92).[\[141\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-141)

The _special issue_ [Canadian Gold Maple Leaf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian_Gold_Maple_Leaf "Canadian Gold Maple Leaf") coin contains the highest purity gold of any [bullion coin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bullion_coin "Bullion coin"), at 99.999% or 0.99999, while the _popular issue_ Canadian Gold Maple Leaf coin has a purity of 99.99%. In 2006, the [United States Mint](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Mint "United States Mint") began producing the [American Buffalo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Buffalo_(coin) "American Buffalo (coin)") gold bullion coin with a purity of 99.99%. The [Australian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia "Australia") Gold Kangaroos were first coined in 1986 as the [Australian Gold Nugget](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australian_Gold_Nugget "Australian Gold Nugget") but changed the reverse design in 1989. Other modern coins include the [Austrian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Austria "Austria") [Vienna Philharmonic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vienna_Philharmonic_(coin) "Vienna Philharmonic (coin)") bullion coin and the [Chinese Gold Panda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_Gold_Panda "Chinese Gold Panda").[\[142\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-142)

### Price

Further information: [Gold as an investment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_as_an_investment "Gold as an investment") and [History of gold § Price](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_gold#Price "History of gold")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Gold_price_in_USD.png/500px-Gold_price_in_USD.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gold_price_in_USD.png) Gold price history in 1960–present.

Like other precious metals, gold is measured by [troy weight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Troy_weight "Troy weight") and by grams. The proportion of gold in the alloy is measured by _[karat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fineness#karat "Fineness")_ (k), with 24 karat (24k) being pure gold (100%), and lower karat numbers proportionally less (18k = 75%). The purity of a [gold bar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_bar "Gold bar") or coin can also be expressed as a decimal figure ranging from 0 to 1, known as the [millesimal fineness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fineness#millesimal_fineness "Fineness"), such as 0.995 being nearly pure.

The price of gold is determined through trading in the gold and [derivatives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative_(finance) "Derivative (finance)") markets, but a procedure known as the [Gold Fixing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_Fixing "Gold Fixing") in [London](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/London "London"), originating in September 1919, provides a daily benchmark price to the industry. The afternoon fixing was introduced in 1968 to provide a price when US markets are open.[\[143\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-143)

## Other applications

### Jewelry

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/CairoEgMuseumTaaMaskMostlyPhotographed.jpg/250px-CairoEgMuseumTaaMaskMostlyPhotographed.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:CairoEgMuseumTaaMaskMostlyPhotographed.jpg) [Tutankhamun's gold funerary mask](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mask_of_Tutankhamun "Mask of Tutankhamun") at the [Egyptian Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egyptian_Museum "Egyptian Museum") in [Cairo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cairo "Cairo"), [Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egypt "Egypt") in 2016

Pure (24k) gold is often [alloyed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alloy "Alloy") with other metals for use in jewelry, altering its hardness and ductility, melting point, color and other properties. Alloys with lower [karat rating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fineness#Karat "Fineness"), typically 22k, 18k, 14k or 10k, contain higher percentages of copper, silver, or other base metals in the alloy.[\[33\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-WorldGoldCouncil-33) Nickel is toxic, and its release from nickel white gold is controlled by legislation in Europe.[\[33\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-WorldGoldCouncil-33) Palladium-gold alloys are more expensive than those using nickel.[\[144\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-144) High-karat white gold alloys are more resistant to corrosion than are either pure silver or [sterling silver](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sterling_silver "Sterling silver"), though not as corrosion-proof as [platinum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platinum "Platinum") jewelry. The Japanese craft of [Mokume-gane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mokume-gane "Mokume-gane") exploits the color contrasts between laminated colored gold alloys to produce decorative wood-grain effects.

Gold [solder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solder "Solder") is used for joining the components of gold jewelry by high-temperature hard soldering or [brazing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brazing "Brazing"). If the work is to be of [hallmarking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hallmark "Hallmark") quality, the gold solder alloy must match the fineness of the work, and alloy formulas are manufactured to color-match yellow and white gold. Gold solder is usually made in at least three melting-point ranges referred to as Easy, Medium and Hard. By using the hard, high-melting point solder first, followed by solders with progressively lower melting points, goldsmiths can assemble complex items with several separate soldered joints. Gold can also be made into [thread](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_thread "Gold thread") and used in [embroidery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embroidery "Embroidery").

### Electronics

Only 10% of the world consumption of new gold produced goes to industry,[\[16\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Soos-2011-16) but by far the most important industrial use for new gold is in fabrication of corrosion-free [electrical connectors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_connectors "Electrical connectors") in computers and other electrical devices. For example, according to the World Gold Council, a typical cell phone may contain 50 mg of gold, worth about three dollars. But since nearly one billion cell phones are produced each year, a gold value of US$2.82 (equivalent to $3 in 2025) in each phone adds to US$2.82 billion in gold from just this application (equivalent to $3,102,513,077 in 2025).[\[145\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-145)

Though gold is attacked by free chlorine, its good conductivity and general resistance to oxidation and corrosion in other environments (including resistance to non-chlorinated acids) has led to its widespread industrial use in the electronic era as a thin-layer coating on [electrical connectors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_connector "Electrical connector"), thereby ensuring good connection. For example, gold is used in the connectors of the more expensive electronics cables, such as audio, video and [USB](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/USB "USB") cables. The benefit of using gold over other connector metals such as [tin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tin "Tin") in these applications has been debated; gold connectors are often criticized by audio-visual experts as unnecessary for most consumers and seen as simply a marketing ploy. However, the use of gold in other applications in electronic sliding contacts in highly humid or corrosive atmospheres, and in use for contacts with a very high failure cost (certain [computers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer "Computer"), communications equipment, [spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spacecraft "Spacecraft"), [jet aircraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jet_aircraft "Jet aircraft") engines) remains very common.[\[146\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-146)

Besides sliding electrical contacts, gold is also used in [electrical contacts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_contacts "Electrical contacts") because of its resistance to [corrosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corrosion "Corrosion"), [electrical conductivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_conductivity "Electrical conductivity"), [ductility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ductile "Ductile") and lack of [toxicity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toxicity "Toxicity").[\[147\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-147) Switch contacts are generally subjected to more intense corrosion stress than are sliding contacts. Fine gold wires are used to connect [semiconductor devices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semiconductor_device "Semiconductor device") to their packages through a process known as [wire bonding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wire_bonding "Wire bonding").

The concentration of free electrons in gold metal is 5.91×1022 cm−3.[\[148\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-148) Gold is highly [conductive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_conductivity "Electrical conductivity") to electricity and has been used for [electrical wiring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_wiring "Electrical wiring") in some high-energy applications (only silver and copper are more conductive per volume, but gold is the only of these three with zero corrosion). For example, gold electrical wires were used during some of the [Manhattan Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manhattan_Project "Manhattan Project")'s atomic experiments, but large high-current silver wires were used in the [calutron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calutron "Calutron") isotope separator magnets in the project.

It is estimated that 16% of the world's presently-accounted-for gold and 22% of the world's silver is contained in electronic technology in Japan.[\[149\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-149)

### Medicine

There are only two gold compounds currently employed as pharmaceuticals in modern medicine ( [sodium aurothiomalate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium_aurothiomalate "Sodium aurothiomalate") and [auranofin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auranofin "Auranofin")), used in the treatment of arthritis and other similar conditions in the US due to their [anti-inflammatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-inflammatory "Anti-inflammatory") properties. These drugs have been explored as a means to help to reduce the pain and swelling of [rheumatoid arthritis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rheumatoid_arthritis "Rheumatoid arthritis"), and also (historically) against [tuberculosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tuberculosis "Tuberculosis") and some parasites.[\[150\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Messori-2004-150)[\[151\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-mech08-151)

Historically, metallic and gold compounds have long been used for medicinal purposes. Gold, usually as the metal, is perhaps the most anciently administered medicine (apparently by shamanic practitioners)[\[151\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-mech08-151) and known to [Dioscorides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dioscorides "Dioscorides").[\[152\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-152)[\[153\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-153) In medieval times, gold was often seen as beneficial for the health, in the belief that something so rare and beautiful could not be anything but healthy.

In the 19th century gold had a reputation as an [anxiolytic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anxiolytic "Anxiolytic"), a therapy for nervous disorders. [Depression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Depression_(mood) "Depression (mood)"), [epilepsy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epilepsy "Epilepsy"), [migraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Migraine "Migraine"), and glandular problems such as [amenorrhea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amenorrhea "Amenorrhea") and [impotence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impotence "Impotence") were treated, and most notably [alcoholism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alcoholism "Alcoholism") (Keeley, 1897).[\[154\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-154)

The apparent paradox\[ _[further explanation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify "Wikipedia:Please clarify")_\] of the actual toxicology of the substance suggests the possibility of serious gaps in the understanding of the action of gold in physiology.[\[155\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-155) Only salts and radioisotopes of gold are of pharmacological value, since elemental (metallic) gold is inert to all chemicals it encounters inside the body (e.g., ingested gold cannot be attacked by stomach acid).

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Gold255.jpg/250px-Gold255.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gold255.jpg) Colloidal gold varies in color with the size of gold particles

Gold alloys are used in [restorative dentistry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Restorative_dentistry "Restorative dentistry"), especially in tooth restorations, such as [crowns](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crown_(dental_restoration) "Crown (dental restoration)") and permanent [bridges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bridge_(dentistry) "Bridge (dentistry)"). The gold alloys' slight malleability facilitates the creation of a superior molar mating surface with other teeth and produces results that are generally more satisfactory than those produced by the creation of porcelain crowns. The use of gold crowns in more prominent teeth such as incisors is favored in some cultures and discouraged in others.

[Colloidal gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colloidal_gold "Colloidal gold") preparations (suspensions of [gold nanoparticles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_nanoparticle "Gold nanoparticle")) in water are intensely red- [colored](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Color "Color"), and can be made with tightly controlled particle sizes up to a few tens of nanometers across by reduction of gold chloride with [citrate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Citrate "Citrate") or [ascorbate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ascorbate "Ascorbate") ions. Colloidal gold is used in research applications in medicine, biology and [materials science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Materials_science "Materials science"). The technique of [immunogold labeling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immunogold_labeling "Immunogold labeling") exploits the ability of the gold particles to adsorb protein molecules onto their surfaces. Colloidal gold particles coated with specific antibodies can be used as probes for the presence and position of antigens on the surfaces of cells.[\[156\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-156) In ultrathin sections of tissues viewed by [electron microscopy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_microscope "Electron microscope"), the immunogold labels appear as extremely dense round spots at the position of the [antigen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigen "Antigen").[\[157\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-157)

Gold, or alloys of gold and [palladium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palladium "Palladium"), are applied as conductive coating to biological specimens and other non-conducting materials such as plastics and glass to be viewed in a [scanning electron microscope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscope "Scanning electron microscope"). The coating, which is usually applied by [sputtering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sputtering "Sputtering") with an [argon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argon "Argon") [plasma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plasma_(physics) "Plasma (physics)"), has a triple role in this application. Gold's very high electrical conductivity drains [electrical charge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_charge "Electric charge") to earth, and its very high density provides stopping power for electrons in the [electron beam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_beam "Electron beam"), helping to limit the depth to which the electron beam penetrates the specimen. This improves definition of the position and topography of the specimen surface and increases the [spatial resolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_resolution "Angular resolution") of the image. Gold also produces a high output of [secondary electrons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_emission "Secondary emission") when irradiated by an electron beam, and these low-energy electrons are the most commonly used signal source used in the scanning electron microscope.[\[158\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-158)

The isotope [gold-198](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold-198 "Gold-198") ( [half-life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Half-life "Half-life") 2.7 days) is used in [nuclear medicine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_medicine "Nuclear medicine"), in some [cancer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cancer "Cancer") treatments and for treating other diseases.[\[159\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-159)[\[160\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-160)

### Cuisine

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Dessert_%2810938449815%29.jpg/330px-Dessert_%2810938449815%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Dessert_(10938449815).jpg)

[File:Dessert (10938449815).jpg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Dessert_(10938449815).jpg "File:Dessert (10938449815).jpg")

Cake with edible gold decoration

Gold can be used in food and has the [E number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E_number "E number") 175.[\[161\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-FSA-2007-161) It can be applied as [gold leaf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_leaf "Gold leaf"), flake or dust for decorative purposes. Since metallic gold is generally considered inert to all body chemistry, it has no taste, provides no nutrition, and leaves the body unaltered.[\[162\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-KingUses-162) In 2016, the [European Food Safety Authority](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority "European Food Safety Authority") published an opinion on the re-evaluation of gold as a food additive. Concerns included the possible presence of minute amounts of gold [nanoparticles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoparticle "Nanoparticle") in the food additive, and that gold nanoparticles have been shown to be [genotoxic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genotoxic "Genotoxic") in mammalian cells [in vitro](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/In_vitro "In vitro").[\[163\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-163)

Decorative use of gold flake goes back to [medieval Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medieval_Europe "Medieval Europe") as a decoration in food and drinks among nobility.[\[164\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-164) Leaf or flakes are used today in sweets and drinks.[\[165\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-165) [Vark](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vark "Vark") is a foil or leaf composed of a pure metal that can include gold,[\[166\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-166) and is used for [garnishing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Garnish_(food) "Garnish (food)") sweets in South Asian cuisine.

Danziger [Goldwasser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goldwasser "Goldwasser") (English: Goldwater) is a traditional German herbal [liqueur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liqueur "Liqueur")[\[167\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-167) produced in what is today [Gdańsk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk "Gdańsk"), [Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poland "Poland"), and [Schwabach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schwabach "Schwabach"), Germany, and contains flakes of gold leaf. There are also some expensive (~ $1,000, equivalent to $1,501 in 2025) cocktails which contain flakes of gold leaf.[\[162\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-KingUses-162)\[ _[verification needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability "Wikipedia:Verifiability")_\]

### Miscellanea

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/James_Webb_Space_Telescope_Mirror33.jpg/250px-James_Webb_Space_Telescope_Mirror33.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:James_Webb_Space_Telescope_Mirror33.jpg) A [mirror segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_Telescope_Element "Optical Telescope Element") for the [James Webb Space Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Webb_Space_Telescope "James Webb Space Telescope") coated in gold to reflect infrared light

- Gold and its salts produce a deep, intense red color when used as a coloring agent in [cranberry glass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cranberry_glass "Cranberry glass").
- In photography, gold toners are used to shift the color of [silver bromide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silver_bromide "Silver bromide") black-and-white prints towards brown or blue tones, or to increase their stability. Used on [sepia-toned](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sepia_tone "Sepia tone") prints, gold toners produce red tones. Kodak published formulas for several types of gold toners, which use gold as the chloride.[\[168\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-168)
- Gold is a good reflector of [electromagnetic radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation "Electromagnetic radiation") such as [infrared](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared "Infrared") and [visible light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visible_spectrum "Visible spectrum"), as well as [radio waves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radio_frequency "Radio frequency"). It is used for the protective coatings on many artificial [satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satellite "Satellite"), in infrared protective faceplates in thermal-protection suits and astronauts' helmets, and in [electronic warfare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_warfare "Electronic warfare") planes such as the [EA-6B Prowler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EA-6B_Prowler "EA-6B Prowler").
- Gold is used as the reflective layer on some [high-end CDs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_CD "Gold CD").
- Automobiles may use gold for heat shielding. [McLaren](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/McLaren "McLaren") uses gold foil in the engine compartment of its [F1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/McLaren_F1 "McLaren F1") model.[\[169\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-169)
- Gold can be manufactured so thin that it appears semi-transparent. It is used in some aircraft cockpit windows for [de-icing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deicing "Deicing") or anti-icing by passing electricity through it. The heat produced by the resistance of the gold is enough to prevent ice from forming.[\[170\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-GoldBulletin-170)
- Gold is attacked by and dissolves in alkaline solutions of potassium or sodium [cyanide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanide "Cyanide"), to form the salt gold cyanide—a technique that has been used in extracting metallic gold from ores in the [cyanide process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanide_process "Cyanide process"). Gold cyanide is the [electrolyte](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrolyte "Electrolyte") used in commercial [electroplating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electroplating "Electroplating") of gold onto base metals and [electroforming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electroforming "Electroforming").
- Gold chloride ( [chloroauric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chloroauric_acid "Chloroauric acid")) solutions are used to make colloidal gold by reduction with [citrate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Citrate "Citrate") or [ascorbate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ascorbate "Ascorbate") [ions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ions "Ions"). Gold chloride and gold oxide are used to make cranberry or red-colored glass, which, like [colloidal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colloid "Colloid") gold suspensions, contains evenly sized spherical [gold nanoparticles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_nanoparticle "Gold nanoparticle").[\[171\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-171)
- Gold, when dispersed in nanoparticles, can act as a [heterogeneous catalyst](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heterogeneous_gold_catalysis "Heterogeneous gold catalysis") of chemical reactions.
- In recent years, gold has been used as a symbol of pride by the [autism rights movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autism_rights_movement "Autism rights movement"), as its symbol Au could be seen as similar to the word " [autism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autism_spectrum_disorder "Autism spectrum disorder")".[\[172\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-172)

## Toxicity

Pure metallic (elemental) gold is non-toxic and non-irritating when ingested[\[173\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-173) and is sometimes used as a food decoration in the form of [gold leaf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_leaf "Gold leaf").[\[174\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-174) Metallic gold is also a component of the alcoholic drinks [Goldschläger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goldschl%C3%A4ger "Goldschläger"), [Gold Strike](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_Strike_(drink) "Gold Strike (drink)"), and [Goldwasser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goldwasser "Goldwasser"). Metallic gold is approved as a [food additive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_additive "Food additive") in the EU ( [E175](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E_number "E number") in the [Codex Alimentarius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Codex_Alimentarius "Codex Alimentarius")). Although the gold ion is toxic, the acceptance of metallic gold as a food additive is due to its relative chemical inertness, and resistance to being corroded or transformed into soluble salts (gold compounds) by any known chemical process which would be encountered in the human body.

Soluble compounds ( [gold salts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_salts "Gold salts")) such as [gold chloride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold(I,III)_chloride "Gold(I,III) chloride") are toxic to the liver and kidneys. Common [cyanide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanide "Cyanide") salts of gold such as potassium gold cyanide, used in gold [electroplating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electroplating "Electroplating"), are toxic by virtue of both their cyanide and gold content. There are rare cases of lethal gold poisoning from [potassium gold cyanide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potassium_gold_cyanide "Potassium gold cyanide").[\[175\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-175)[\[176\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-176) Gold toxicity can be ameliorated with [chelation therapy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chelation_therapy "Chelation therapy") with an agent such as [dimercaprol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dimercaprol "Dimercaprol").

Gold metal was voted [Allergen of the Year](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allergen_of_the_Year "Allergen of the Year") in 2001 by the American Contact Dermatitis Society; gold contact allergies affect mostly women.[\[177\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-Tsuruta-2001-177) Despite this, gold is a relatively non-potent contact allergen, in comparison with metals like [nickel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nickel "Nickel").[\[178\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-178)

A sample of the fungus _[Aspergillus niger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aspergillus_niger "Aspergillus niger")_ was found growing from gold mining solution; and was found to contain cyano metal complexes, such as gold, silver, copper, iron and zinc. The fungus also plays a role in the solubilization of heavy metal sulfides.[\[179\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_note-179)

032. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold#cite_ref-32 "Jump up")**Pyykkö, Pekka (27 August 2004). ["Theoretical Chemistry of Gold"](https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/anie.200300624). _Angewandte Chemie International Edition_. **43** (34): 4412–4456\. [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"): [10.1002/anie.200300624](https://doi.org/10.1002%2Fanie.200300624). [ISSN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISSN_(identifier) "ISSN (identifier)") [1433-7851](https://search.worldcat.org/issn/1433-7851). Finally, the attribution of the yellow color of gold to relativity rests on the result that the relativistic interband energy between the top of the 5d band and the Fermi level in the half-filled 6s band, 2.4 eV, is close to the experimental value of 2.38 eV, whereas the NR interband energy would be much larger. Comparative R/NR calculations of the reflectivity do not seem to exist. Similarly it would seem that the exact roles of the bulk electronic-band structure and that of surface plasmons have not yet been worked out.

## Further reading

- Bachmann, H. G. _The lure of gold : an artistic and cultural history_ (2006) [online](https://archive.org/details/lureofgold0000unse)
- Bernstein, Peter L. _The Power of Gold: The History of an Obsession_ (2000) [online](https://archive.org/details/powerofgoldhisto00bern)
- Brands, H.W. _The Age of Gold: The California Gold Rush and the New American Dream_ (2003) [excerpt](https://www.amazon.com/Age-Gold-California-American-Recover/dp/0385720882/)
- Buranelli, Vincent. _Gold : an illustrated history_ (1979) [online](https://archive.org/details/goldillustratedh00bura)' wide-ranging popular history
- Cassel, Gustav. "The restoration of the gold standard." _Economica_ 9 (1923): 171–185. [online](https://www.jstor.org/stable/2548130)
- Eichengreen, Barry. _Golden Fetters: The Gold Standard and the Great Depression, 1919–1939_ (Oxford UP, 1992).
- Ferguson, Niall. _The Ascent of Money – Financial History of the World_ (2009) [online](https://archive.org/details/ascentofmoneyf00ferg)
- Hart, Matthew, [Gold: The Race for the World's Most Seductive Metal](https://books.google.com/books?id=kSI5AAAAQBAJ) _Gold : the race for the world's most seductive metal", New York: Simon & Schuster, 2013. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9781451650020](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781451650020 "Special:BookSources/9781451650020")_
- Johnson, Harry G (1969). "The gold rush of 1968 in retrospect and prospect". _American Economic Review_. **59** (2): 344–348\. [JSTOR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier) "JSTOR (identifier)") [1823687](https://www.jstor.org/stable/1823687).
- Kwarteng, Kwasi. _War and Gold: A Five-Hundred-Year History of Empires, Adventures, and Debt_ (2014) [online](https://archive.org/details/wargoldfivehundr0000kwar)
- Vilar, Pierre. _A History of Gold and Money, 1450–1920_ (1960). [online](https://archive.org/details/historyofgoldmon0000vila_c6t2)
- Vilches, Elvira. _New World Gold: Cultural Anxiety and Monetary Disorder in Early Modern Spain_ (2010).

## Uses and properties

[Help](https://periodic-table.rsc.org/Help/Uses "Help")

## Uses and properties

[Help](https://periodic-table.rsc.org/Help/Uses "Help")

**Image explanation**

In this image a traditional alchemical symbol for the element is used. It is also used as a sun symbol, and much of the mythology around gold relates to the sun. Early alchemists were obsessed by gold and pursued their desire to transmute base metals (usually lead) into gold. The image in the background is based on a symbolic representation of an alchemist’s ‘laboratory’.


**Appearance**

A soft metal with a characteristic yellow colour. It is chemically unreactive, although it will dissolve in aqua regia (a mixture of nitric and hydrochloric acids).


**Uses**

Most mined gold is stored as bullion. It is also, however, used extensively in jewellery, either in its pure form or as an alloy. The term ‘carat’ indicates the amount of gold present in an alloy. 24-carat is pure gold, but it is very soft. 18- and 9-carat gold alloys are commonly used because they are more durable.

The metal is also used for coinage, and has been used as standard for monetary systems in some countries.

Gold can be beaten into very thin sheets (gold leaf) to be used in art, for decoration and as architectural ornament. Electroplating can be used to cover another metal with a very thin layer of gold. This is used in gears for watches, artificial limb joints, cheap jewellery and electrical connectors. It is ideal for protecting electrical copper components because it conducts electricity well and does not corrode (which would break the contact). Thin gold wires are used inside computer chips to produce circuits.

Dentists sometimes use gold alloys in fillings, and a gold compound is used to treat some cases of arthritis.

Gold nanoparticles are increasingly being used as industrial catalysts. Vinyl acetate, which is used to make PVA (for glue, paint and resin), is made using a gold catalyst.

**Biological role**

Gold has no known biological role, and is non-toxic.


**Natural abundance**

Gold is one of the few elements to occur in a natural state. It is found in veins and alluvial deposits. About 1500 tonnes of gold are mined each year. About two-thirds of this comes from South Africa and most of the rest from Russia.

Seawater contains about 4 grams of gold in 1,000,000 tonnes of water. Overall this is a huge amount of gold stored in the oceans but, because the concentration is so low, attempts to reclaim this gold have always failed.

## History

## History

[Elements and Periodic Table History](https://periodic-table.rsc.org/history "Elements and Periodic Table History")

Gold has been known since prehistoric times and was one of the first metals to be worked, mainly because it was to be found as nuggets or as particles in the beds of streams. Such was the demand that by 2000 BC the Egyptians began mining gold. The death mask of Tutankhamen, who died in 1323 BC, contained 100 kg of the metal. The royal graves of ancient Ur (modern Iraq), which flourished from 3800 to 2000 BC, also contained gold objects.

The minting of gold coins began around 640 BC in the Kingdom of Lydia (situated in what is now modern Turkey) using electrum, a native alloy of gold and silver. The first pure gold coins were minted in the reign of King Croesus, who ruled from 561–547 BC.

| Transcript :<br> <br>## Chemistry in its element: gold<br>**_(Promo)_**<br>You're listening to Chemistry in its element brought to you by _Chemistry World_, the magazine of the Royal Society of Chemistry.<br>**_(End promo)_**<br>**Chris Smith**<br>Hello, in this week's episode of Chemistry in its element, we're taking a flight on Concorde, dropping by Buckingham Palace and finding out what could form a film just 230 atoms thick. Going for gold for us this week, here's the legendary science broadcaster and populariser Johnny Ball. <br>**Johnny Ball**<br>The element gold. Gold is element 79 and its symbol is Au. Though the name is Anglo Saxon, gold originated from the Latin Aurum, or shining dawn, and previously from the Greek. It's abundance in the earth's crust is 0.004 ppm. <br>100% of gold found naturally is isotope Au-197. 28 other isotopes can be produced artificially and are all radioactive.<br>Gold along with silver and copper, form a column in the periodic table. They are found naturally and were the first three elements known to man. They were all used as primitive money well before the first gold coins which appeared in Egypt around 3400 BC.<br>Most gold is ancient or comes from Central American Aztecs and South American Incas brought to Europe by the Spanish and Portuguese in the 16th century, and which has since been recycled over and over again. In 1830 world output was no more than 12 tonnes per annum. But around that time, new gold discoveries were being made. Finds were discovered in Siberia, California, New South Wales and Victoria, Australia, Transvaal, South Africa, the Klondike and Alaska, and they all produced gold rushes. World production was then around 150 tonnes per year. It is now around 2300 tones per annum.<br>Because it is found in it's natural state and does not naturally alloy with anything else and because it is the heaviest metal, by sifting rock in water, the gold always falls to the bottom and all less dense impurities are washed away. <br>The largest nugget was the Welcome Stranger nugget found in Victoria, Australia in 1869. It weighed over 71 kg. This type of nugget occurs naturally, but is very, very rare. Pure gold is 24 karat. 18 karat is 75% and 12 karat is 50% pure gold. <br>Gold is the most malleable of all metals and soft enough to be cut with a knife. Stone age peoples hammered gold into plates for ornamental purposes. Really quite large amounts were gathered together. Though King Tutankhamun was a minor Pharaoh and died aged 18, his coffin alone contained 112 kg of gold. Egyptians also made thin gold sheets, utensils, vast varieties of jewellery and even gold thread. King Tut when he was buried had over 150 gold ornaments on his body.<br>Today 1 gram can be beaten into a square metre sheet just 230 atoms thick. 1 cubic centimetre would make a sheet of 18 square metres. Concord's windscreen had a layer of gold to screen pilots from UV light and today it is often used in sky scraper windows to cut down both heat and UV from sunlight. 1 gram can be drawn to make 165 metres of wire 20 um (microns) thick (1/200th of a millimeter)<br>The gold colour in the Buckingham Palace fence is actually gold covered, as it lasts 30 years, whereas gold paint (which contains no gold at all) lasts in tip top condition, only about a year. <br>Sea water contains around 3 parts in a billion of gold, but there's never been found an economic means of recovering it. The Germans tried very hard during the second World War but failed miserably. <br>The largest modern hoard is the 30,000 tons in the US Federal Reserve Bank in New York, which belongs to 18 different nations. It is estimated that all the world's gold gathered together would only make a cube around 18 metres per side - about 6000 cubic metres. And that's gold.<br>**Chris Smith**<br>So now you know why pirates used to bite gold coins to see if they were real. It wasn't just for the camera because it looked good, it was because the metal was soft enough to be marked by teeth. That was Johnny Ball telling the story of gold. Next time on Chemistry in its element Victoria Gill introduces the chemical that founded the science of photography and also helped to launch the careers of successions of Oscar winners. <br>**Victoria Gill**<br>in 1840, Henry Talbot discovered an additional chemical twist, that a so called latent silver image, that had been briefly exposed onto a layer of silver iodide could be revealed using gallic acid. The effect was seen as magical, a devilish art. Hollywood could never have existed without the chemical reaction that gave celluloid film its ability to capture the stars and bring them to the aptly dubbed silver screen. <br>**Chris Smith**<br>And you can hear Victoria Gill crossing your cognitive palm and lining your intellectual pocket with silver on next week's Chemistry in its element. I'm Chris Smith, thank you for listening, see you next time.<br>**_(Promo)_**<br>Chemistry in its element is brought to you by the Royal Society of Chemistry and produced by [thenakedscientists.com](http://www.thenakedscientists.com/ ""). # Why Is Gold Au: Did You Know?

![U.S. Money Reserve Logo](https://www.usmoneyreserve.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/wp-featured-image.jpeg)

Jan 31, 2020

[Watch and listen to learn about the origin of the symbol for gold “Au,” why the atomic number for gold is 79, and where the word gold comes from.](https://www.usmoneyreserve.com/resources/videos/transcripts/)

Where the Chemical Symbol AU Comes From: Did You Know? - YouTube

Tap to unmute

[Where the Chemical Symbol AU Comes From: Did You Know?](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=UFFTyW871Qs) [U.S. Money Reserve](https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCuL8a3rLDa5aM7_RH0t73-A)

U.S. Money Reserve11.9K subscribers

[Watch on](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=UFFTyW871Qs)

### Where the Chemical Symbol AU Comes From: Did You Know? – Video Transcription

**00:15** \[inaudible\].

**Narrator: [00:16](file:///C:/Users/KRodriguez/Downloads/012320_WhyIsGoldAu-DidYouKnow.pdf)** Gold is a chemical element with the atomic number 79 on the periodic table. It has one of the highest atomic numbers of any element that occurs naturally. An atomic number measures the number of protons contained in the nucleus of each atom comprising an element. The higher an element's atomic number, the heavier it will be. This is why gold is so dense and heavy. Gold is one of the least reactive and most solid chemical elements. It is also the most malleable of all metals. In fact, a single ounce of gold could be compressed into a thin sheet that would span a total of 300 square feet. When formed into a monotonic wire, it can be stretched about twice before it would break, making it optimal for microelectronics and other applications. Gold's chemical symbol AU is derived from the Latin word aurum, meaning gold. Some claim that the word aurum also means shining dawn due to its etymological ties to the word aurora, meaning dawn. The word gold comes from the old English word for yellow, geolu, which was derived from the Germanic word gulþa. Today, gold is everywhere, and in many of the devices we use on a daily basis. Whether you're looking to diversify your portfolio or secure your assets for future generations, U.S. Money Reserve is here to make gold buying easy. Call the number on your screen if you'd like to learn more about gold and other precious metals.

**01:52:** \[inaudible\].

Title: Atom of Gold - Atomic number 79 and chemical symbol Au. Credit to ...
Gold is a chemical element with the symbol Au and atomic number 79. This makes it one of the higher–atomic-number elements that occur naturally.
